<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Nitrous oxide</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> (formula NO), <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), or generic nitrogen oxide pollutants <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses of the terms, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_(disambiguation)" title="Nitrous oxide (disambiguation)">nitrous oxide (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Laughing_gas_(disambiguation)" title="Laughing gas (disambiguation)">laughing gas (disambiguation)</a></div>

<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox references</a>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Nitrous oxide</b>, commonly known as <b>laughing gas</b> or <b>nitrous</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">oxide of nitrogen</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. At room temperature, it is a colorless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammability" title="Flammability">non-flammable</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, with a slight metallic scent and taste. At elevated temperatures, nitrous oxide is a powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">oxidizer</a> similar to molecular oxygen.
It is soluble in water.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide has significant <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_(medication)" title="Nitrous oxide (medication)">medical uses</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a>, for its <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anaesthetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">pain reducing</a> effects. Its name "laughing gas", coined by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Or John Davy? https://www.um.edu.mt/library/oar/bitstream/handle/123456789/20208/Mediscope%2009%20-%20A5.pdf?sequence=1&amp;isAllowed=y (July 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> is due to the <a href="/wiki/Euphoria" title="Euphoria">euphoric</a> effects upon inhaling it, a property that has led to its <a href="/wiki/Recreational_use_of_nitrous_oxide" title="Recreational use of nitrous oxide">recreational use</a> as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissociative_drug" title="Dissociative drug">dissociative</a> anaesthetic. It is on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization%27s_List_of_Essential_Medicines" title="World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines">World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines</a>, the most effective and safe medicines needed in a health system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It also is used as an oxidizer in <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">rocket propellants</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Auto_racing" title="Auto racing">motor racing</a> to increase the power output of <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">engines</a>.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide occurs in small amounts in the atmosphere, but recently has been found to be a major scavenger of <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">stratospheric ozone</a>, with an impact comparable to that of <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>. It is estimated that 30% of the <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> in the atmosphere is the result of human activity, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciozo_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciozo-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocket_motors">Rocket motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Rocket motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitrous oxide may be used as an <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizer</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> motor. This is advantageous over other oxidisers in that it is much less toxic, but due to its stability at room temperature is also easier to store and relatively safe to carry on a flight. As a secondary benefit, it may be decomposed readily to form breathing air. Its high density and low storage pressure (when maintained at low temperature) enable it to be highly competitive with stored high-pressure gas systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 1914 patent, American rocket pioneer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Goddard" title="Robert Goddard">Robert Goddard</a> suggested nitrous oxide and gasoline as possible propellants for a liquid-fuelled rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Nitrous oxide has been the oxidiser of choice in several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_rocket" title="Hybrid rocket">hybrid rocket</a> designs (using solid fuel with a liquid or gaseous oxidizer). The combination of nitrous oxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl-terminated_polybutadiene" title="Hydroxyl-terminated polybutadiene">hydroxyl-terminated polybutadiene</a> fuel has been used by <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a> and others. It also is notably used in <a href="/wiki/Amateur_rocketry" title="Amateur rocketry">amateur</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_power_rocket" title="High power rocket">high power rocketry</a> with various plastics as the fuel.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide also may be used in a <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant rocket</a>. In the presence of a heated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> will decompose exothermically into nitrogen and oxygen, at a temperature of approximately 1,070 °F (577 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Because of the large heat release, the catalytic action rapidly becomes secondary, as thermal autodecomposition becomes dominant. In a vacuum thruster, this may provide a monopropellant <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a> (<i>I</i><sub>sp</sub>) of as much as 180 s. While noticeably less than the <i>I</i><sub>sp</sub> available from <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> thrusters (monopropellant or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant_rocket" title="Bipropellant rocket">bipropellant</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dinitrogen_tetroxide" title="Dinitrogen tetroxide">dinitrogen tetroxide</a>), the decreased toxicity makes nitrous oxide an option worth investigating.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide is said to <a href="/wiki/Deflagration" title="Deflagration">deflagrate</a> at approximately 600 °C (1,112 °F) at a pressure of 309 psi (21 atmospheres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munke_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munke-7">[7]</a></sup> At 600 <abbr title="pounds per square inch">psi</abbr> for example, the required ignition energy is only 6 joules, whereas <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> at 130 psi a 2500-joule ignition energy input is insufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_combustion_engine">Internal combustion engine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Internal combustion engine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_engine" title="Nitrous oxide engine">Nitrous oxide engine</a></div>
<p>In vehicle <a href="/wiki/Racing" title="Racing">racing</a>, nitrous oxide (often referred to as just "<a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_engine" title="Nitrous oxide engine">nitrous</a>") allows the engine to burn more fuel by providing more oxygen than air alone, resulting in a more powerful combustion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The gas is not flammable at a low pressure/temperature, but it delivers more <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> than atmospheric air by breaking down at elevated temperatures. Therefore, it often is mixed with another fuel that is easier to deflagrate. Nitrous oxide is a strong oxidant, roughly equivalent to hydrogen peroxide, and much stronger than oxygen gas.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide is stored as a compressed liquid; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporization" title="Heat of vaporization">evaporation</a> and expansion of liquid nitrous oxide in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intake_manifold" title="Intake manifold">intake manifold</a> causes a large drop in intake charge temperature, resulting in a denser charge, further allowing more air/fuel mixture to enter the cylinder. Sometimes nitrous oxide is injected into (or prior to) the intake manifold, whereas other systems directly inject, right before the cylinder (direct port injection) to increase power.
</p><p>The technique was used during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> by <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> aircraft with the <a href="/wiki/GM-1" title="GM-1">GM-1</a> system to boost the power output of <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">aircraft engines</a>. Originally meant to provide the Luftwaffe standard aircraft with superior high-altitude performance, technological considerations limited its use to extremely high altitudes. Accordingly, it was only used by specialized planes such as high-altitude <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance_aircraft" title="Reconnaissance aircraft">reconnaissance aircraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schnellbomber" title="Schnellbomber">high-speed bombers</a>, and high-altitude <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">interceptor aircraft</a>. It sometimes could be found on Luftwaffe aircraft also fitted with another engine-boost system, <a href="/wiki/MW_50" title="MW 50">MW 50</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Water_injection_(engine)" title="Water injection (engine)">water injection</a> for aviation engines that used <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> for its boost capabilities.
</p><p>One of the major problems of using nitrous oxide in a reciprocating engine is that it can produce enough power to damage or destroy the engine. Very large power increases are possible, and if the mechanical structure of the engine is not properly reinforced, the engine may be severely damaged, or destroyed, during this kind of operation. It is very important with nitrous oxide augmentation of <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engines</a> to maintain proper <a href="/wiki/Operating_temperature" title="Operating temperature">operating temperatures</a> and fuel levels to prevent "pre-ignition",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> or "detonation" (sometimes referred to as "knock"). Most problems that are associated with nitrous oxide do not come from mechanical failure due to the power increases. Since nitrous oxide allows a much denser charge into the cylinder, it dramatically increases cylinder pressures. The increased pressure and temperature can cause problems such as melting the piston or valves. It also may crack or warp the piston or head and cause pre-ignition due to uneven heating.
</p><p>Automotive-grade liquid nitrous oxide differs slightly from medical-grade nitrous oxide. A small amount of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is added to prevent substance abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Automotive_gas_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Automotive_gas-11">[11]</a></sup> Multiple washes through a base (such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>) can remove this, decreasing the corrosive properties observed when <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is further oxidised during combustion into <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, making emissions cleaner.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerosol_propellant">Aerosol propellant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Aerosol propellant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N2O_whippets.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1792" data-file-width="2688" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/N2O_whippets.jpg/220px-N2O_whippets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/N2O_whippets.jpg/330px-N2O_whippets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/N2O_whippets.jpg/440px-N2O_whippets.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N2O_whippets.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Food grade <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> <a href="/wiki/Whipped-cream_charger" title="Whipped-cream charger">whippets</a></div></div></div>
<p>The gas is approved for use as a <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additive</a> (also known as E942), specifically as an <a href="/wiki/Aerosol_spray#Aerosol_propellants" title="Aerosol spray">aerosol spray propellant</a>. Its most common uses in this context are in aerosol <a href="/wiki/Whipped_cream" title="Whipped cream">whipped cream</a> canisters and <a href="/wiki/Cooking_spray" title="Cooking spray">cooking sprays</a>.
</p><p>The gas is extremely soluble in fatty compounds. In aerosol whipped cream, it is dissolved in the fatty cream until it leaves the can, when it becomes gaseous and thus creates foam. Used in this way, it produces whipped cream four times the volume of the liquid, whereas whipping air into cream only produces twice the volume. If air were used as a propellant, oxygen would accelerate <a href="/wiki/Rancidification" title="Rancidification">rancidification</a> of the butterfat, but nitrous oxide inhibits such degradation. Carbon dioxide cannot be used for whipped cream because it is acidic in water, which would curdle the cream and give it a seltzer-like "sparkling" sensation.
</p><p>The whipped cream produced with nitrous oxide is unstable, however, and will return to a more liquid state within half an hour to one hour.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Thus, the method is not suitable for decorating food that will not be served immediately.
</p><p>During December 2016, some manufacturers reported a shortage of aerosol whipped creams in the United States due to an explosion at the <a href="/wiki/Air_Liquide" title="Air Liquide">Air Liquide</a> nitrous oxide facility in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> in late August. With a major facility offline, the disruption caused a shortage resulting in the company diverting the supply of nitrous oxide to medical clients rather than to food manufacturing. The shortage came during the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_and_holiday_season" title="Christmas and holiday season">Christmas and holiday season</a> when canned whipped cream use is normally at its highest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_spray" title="Cooking spray">cooking spray</a>, which is made from various types of oils combined with <a href="/wiki/Lecithin" title="Lecithin">lecithin</a> (an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emulsifier" title="Emulsifier">emulsifier</a>), may use nitrous oxide as a <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a>. Other propellants used in cooking spray include food-grade <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_(medication)" title="Nitrous oxide (medication)">Nitrous oxide (medication)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1516" data-file-width="482" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg/100px-N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg/150px-N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg/200px-N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N2O_Medical_Tanks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medical grade <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> tanks used in <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a></div></div></div>
<p>Nitrous oxide has been used in dentistry and surgery, as an anaesthetic and analgesic, since 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_discovery_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_discovery-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early days, the gas was administered through simple inhalers consisting of a breathing bag made of rubber cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-use_in_dentistry_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-use_in_dentistry-14">[14]</a></sup> Today, the gas is administered in hospitals by means of an automated <a href="/wiki/Relative_analgesia_machine" title="Relative analgesia machine">relative analgesia machine</a>, with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic_vaporiser" title="Anaesthetic vaporiser">anaesthetic vaporiser</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Medical_ventilator" title="Medical ventilator">medical ventilator</a>, that delivers a precisely dosed and breath-actuated flow of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_and_oxygen" title="Nitrous oxide and oxygen">nitrous oxide mixed with oxygen</a> in a 2:1 ratio.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide is a weak <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthetic" title="General anaesthetic">general anaesthetic</a>, and so is generally not used alone in general anaesthesia, but used as a carrier gas (mixed with oxygen) for more powerful general anaesthetic drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Sevoflurane" title="Sevoflurane">sevoflurane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Desflurane" title="Desflurane">desflurane</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/Minimum_alveolar_concentration" title="Minimum alveolar concentration">minimum alveolar concentration</a> of 105% and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood/gas_partition_coefficient" title="Blood/gas partition coefficient">blood/gas partition coefficient</a> of 0.46. The use of nitrous oxide in anaesthesia, however, can increase the risk of postoperative nausea and vomiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dentists use a simpler machine, that only delivers a <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>/<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> mixture for the patient to inhale while conscious. The patient is kept conscious throughout the procedure, and retains adequate mental faculties to respond to questions and instructions from the dentist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inhalation of nitrous oxide is used frequently to relieve pain associated with <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_trauma" title="Physical trauma">trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">oral surgery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acute_coronary_syndrome" title="Acute coronary syndrome">acute coronary syndrome</a> (includes heart attacks). Its use during labour has been shown to be a safe and effective aid for birthing women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Its use for acute coronary syndrome is of unknown benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA10_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHA10-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Britain and Canada, Entonox and Nitronox are used commonly by ambulance crews (including unregistered practitioners) as a rapid and highly effective analgesic gas.
</p><p>50% nitrous oxide can be considered for use by trained non-professional first aid responders in prehospital settings, given the relative ease and safety of administering 50% nitrous oxide as an analgesic. The rapid reversibility of its effect would also prevent it from precluding diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreational_use">Recreational use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Recreational use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax,_with_a_party_of_friends,_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3669" data-file-width="5373" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg/220px-Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg/330px-Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg/440px-Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax%2C_with_a_party_of_friends%2C_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doctor_and_Mrs_Syntax,_with_a_party_of_friends,_experimentin_Wellcome_L0022227.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aquatint" title="Aquatint">Aquatint</a> depiction of a laughing gas party in the nineteenth century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school,_Utrecht,_2017_-_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg/220px-Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg/330px-Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg/440px-Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school%2C_Utrecht%2C_2017_-_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nitrous_oxide_whippits_used_recreationally_as_a_drug_by_Dutch_youngsters_near_a_school,_Utrecht,_2017_-_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Whippit remnants of recreational drug use, the Netherlands, 2017</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Recreational_use_of_nitrous_oxide" title="Recreational use of nitrous oxide">Recreational inhalation of nitrous oxide</a>, with the purpose of causing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphoria_(emotion)" title="Euphoria (emotion)">euphoria</a> and/or slight <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a>, began as a phenomenon for the British upper class in 1799, known as "laughing gas parties".
</p><p>Starting in the nineteenth century, widespread availability of the gas for medical and culinary purposes allowed the recreational use to expand greatly, throughout the world. In the United Kingdom, as of 2014, nitrous oxide was estimated to be used by almost half a million young people at nightspots, festivals, and parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The <a href="#Legality">legality</a> of that use varies greatly from country to country, and even from city to city in some countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The major safety hazards of nitrous oxide come from the fact that it is a compressed liquefied gas, an asphyxiation risk, and a <a href="/wiki/Dissociative" title="Dissociative">dissociative</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a>.
</p><p>While relatively non-toxic, nitrous oxide has a number of recognized ill effects on human health, whether through breathing it in or by contact of the liquid with skin or eyes.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide is a significant <a href="/wiki/Occupational_hazard" title="Occupational hazard">occupational hazard</a> for surgeons, dentists, and nurses. Because nitrous oxide is minimally metabolised in humans (with a rate of 0.004%), it retains its potency when exhaled into the room by the patient, and can pose an intoxicating and prolonged exposure hazard to the clinic staff if the room is poorly ventilated. Where nitrous oxide is administered, a continuous-flow fresh-air <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">ventilation system</a> or <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> <a href="/wiki/Scavenger_system" title="Scavenger system">scavenger system</a> is used to prevent a waste-gas buildup.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> recommends that workers' exposure to nitrous oxide should be controlled during the administration of anaesthetic gas in medical, dental, and veterinary operators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 25 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> (46 mg/m<sup>3</sup>) to escaped anaesthetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_and_manual_impairment">Mental and manual impairment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mental and manual impairment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Exposure to nitrous oxide causes short-term decreases in mental performance, audiovisual ability, and manual dexterity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> These effects coupled with the induced spatial and temporal disorientation could result in physical harm to the user from environmental hazards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Jay_22–25_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Jay_22–25-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurotoxicity_and_neuroprotection">Neurotoxicity and neuroprotection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Neurotoxicity and neuroprotection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMDA_antagonist" title="NMDA antagonist">NMDA antagonists</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> was suggested to produce <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxicity</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Olney%27s_lesions" title="Olney's lesions">Olney's lesions</a> in rodents upon prolonged (several hour) exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14622904_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14622904-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12854473_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12854473-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10928976_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10928976-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15718054_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15718054-30">[30]</a></sup> New research has arisen suggesting that Olney's lesions do not occur in humans, however, and similar drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Ketamine" title="Ketamine">ketamine</a> are now believed not to be acutely neurotoxic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cynomolgus_monkey_neurotoxiciy_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cynomolgus_monkey_neurotoxiciy-32">[32]</a></sup> It has been argued that, because <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> has a very short duration under normal circumstances, it is less likely to be neurotoxic than other NMDA antagonists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16179534_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16179534-33">[33]</a></sup> Indeed, in rodents, short-term exposure results in only mild injury that is rapidly reversible, and neuronal death occurs only after constant and sustained exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14622904_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14622904-27">[27]</a></sup> Nitrous oxide also may cause neurotoxicity after extended exposure because of <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>. This is especially true of non-medical formulations such as <a href="/wiki/Whipped-cream_charger" title="Whipped-cream charger">whipped-cream chargers</a> (also known as "whippets" or "nangs"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> which never contain oxygen, since oxygen makes cream rancid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Middleton_2012_p._35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Middleton_2012_p.-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, nitrous oxide depletes <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub><small>12</small></sub></a> levels. This can cause serious neurotoxicity if the user has preexisting <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12_deficiency" title="Vitamin B12 deficiency">vitamin B<sub><small>12</small></sub> deficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nitrous oxide at 75-vol% reduce ischemia-induced neuronal death induced by occlusion of the middle cerebral artery in rodents, and decrease NMDA-induced Ca2+ influx in neuronal cell cultures, a critical event involved in <a href="/wiki/Excitotoxicity" title="Excitotoxicity">excitotoxicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxygen_deprivation">Oxygen deprivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Oxygen deprivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If pure nitrous oxide is inhaled without oxygen mixed in, this can eventually lead to oxygen deprivation resulting in loss of blood pressure, fainting and even heart attacks. This can occur if the user inhales large quantities continuously, as with a strap-on mask connected to a gas canister. It can also happen if the user engages in excessive breath-holding or uses any other inhalation system that cuts off their supply of fresh air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitamin_B12_deficiency">Vitamin B<sub><small>12</small></sub> deficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vitamin B12 deficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Long-term exposure to nitrous oxide may cause <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub><small>12</small></sub></a> deficiency. It inactivates the cobalamin form of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a> by oxidation. Symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12_deficiency" title="Vitamin B12 deficiency">vitamin B<sub>12</sub> deficiency</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_neuropathy" title="Sensory neuropathy">sensory neuropathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myelopathy" title="Myelopathy">myelopathy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Encephalopathy" title="Encephalopathy">encephalopathy</a>, may occur within days or weeks of exposure to nitrous oxide anaesthesia in people with subclinical vitamin B<sub>12</sub> deficiency.
</p><p>Symptoms are treated with high doses of vitamin B<sub>12</sub>, but recovery can be slow and incomplete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>People with normal vitamin B<sub>12</sub> levels have stores to make the effects of nitrous oxide insignificant, unless exposure is repeated and prolonged (nitrous oxide abuse). Vitamin B<sub>12</sub> levels should be checked in people with risk factors for vitamin B<sub>12</sub> deficiency prior to using nitrous oxide anaesthesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prenatal_development">Prenatal development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prenatal development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several experimental studies in rats indicate that chronic exposure of pregnant females to nitrous oxide may have adverse effects on the developing fetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vieira1980_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vieira1980-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vieira1980_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vieira1980-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chemical.2Fphysical_risks"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical/physical_risks">Chemical/physical risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Chemical/physical risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At room temperature (20 °C (68 °F)) the saturated vapour pressure is 50.525 bar, rising up to 72.45 bar at 36.4 °C (97.5 °F)—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a>. The pressure curve is thus unusually sensitive to temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Liquid nitrous oxide acts as a good solvent for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>; liquid mixtures may form shock sensitive explosives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>As with many strong oxidisers, contamination of parts with fuels have been implicated in rocketry accidents, where small quantities of nitrous/fuel mixtures explode due to "water hammer"-like effects (sometimes called "dieseling"—heating due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic" title="Adiabatic">adiabatic</a> compression of gases can reach decomposition temperatures).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Some common building materials such as stainless steel and aluminium can act as fuels with strong oxidisers such as nitrous oxide, as can contaminants that may ignite due to adiabatic compression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>There also have been incidents where nitrous oxide decomposition in plumbing has led to the explosion of large tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munke_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Munke-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The pharmacological <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_of_action" title="Mechanism of action">mechanism of action</a> of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> in medicine is not fully known. However, it has been shown to directly modulate a broad range of <a href="/wiki/Ligand-gated_ion_channel" title="Ligand-gated ion channel">ligand-gated ion channels</a>, and this likely plays a major role in many of its effects. It moderately blocks <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA</a> and <a href="/wiki/CHRNB2" title="CHRNB2">β<sub>2</sub>-subunit</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Nicotinic_acetylcholine_receptor" title="Nicotinic acetylcholine receptor">nACh channels</a>, weakly inhibits <a href="/wiki/AMPA_receptor" title="AMPA receptor">AMPA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kainate_receptor" title="Kainate receptor">kainate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABAC_receptor" title="GABAC receptor">GABA<sub>C</sub></a>, and <a href="/wiki/5-HT3_receptor" title="5-HT3 receptor">5-HT<sub>3</sub> receptors</a>, and slightly potentiates <a href="/wiki/GABAA_receptor" title="GABAA receptor">GABA<sub>A</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycine_receptor" title="Glycine receptor">glycine receptors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11020766_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11020766-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9822732_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9822732-48">[48]</a></sup> It also has been shown to activate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tandem_pore_domain_potassium_channel" title="Tandem pore domain potassium channel">two-pore-domain <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> channels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14742687_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14742687-49">[49]</a></sup> While <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> affects quite a few ion channels, its anaesthetic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hallucinogenic" title="Hallucinogenic">hallucinogenic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphoriant" title="Euphoriant">euphoriant</a> effects are likely caused predominantly, or fully, via inhibition of NMDA receptor-mediated currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11020766_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11020766-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17352529_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17352529-50">[50]</a></sup> In addition to its effects on ion channels, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> may act to imitate <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> (NO) in the central nervous system, and this may be related to its <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anxiolytic" title="Anxiolytic">anxiolytic</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17352529_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17352529-50">[50]</a></sup> Nitrous oxide is 30-40 times more soluble than nitrogen.  
</p><p>The effects of inhaling sub-anaesthetic doses of Nitrous Oxide have been known to vary, based on several factors, including settings and individual differences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> however, from his discussion, Jay (2008)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Jay_22–25_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Jay_22–25-26">[26]</a></sup> suggests that it has been reliably known to induce the following states and sensations:
</p>
<ul><li>Intoxication</li>
<li>Euphoria/dysphoria</li>
<li>Spatial disorientation</li>
<li>Temporal disorientation</li>
<li>Reduced pain sensitivity</li></ul>
<p>A minority of users also will present with uncontrolled vocalisations and muscular spasms. These effects generally disappear minutes after removal of the nitrous oxide source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Jay_22–25_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Jay_22–25-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Euphoric_effect">Euphoric effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Euphoric effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In rats, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> stimulates the <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">mesolimbic reward pathway</a> via inducing <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> release and activating <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">dopaminergic</a> <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmental_area" title="Ventral tegmental area">ventral tegmental area</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a>, presumably through <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor_antagonist" title="NMDA receptor antagonist">antagonisation of NMDA receptors</a> localised in the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17122223_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17122223-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18571333_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18571333-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8726543_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8726543-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18499630_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18499630-56">[56]</a></sup> This action has been implicated in its euphoric effects, and notably, appears to augment its analgesic properties as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17122223_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17122223-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18571333_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18571333-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8726543_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8726543-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18499630_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18499630-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is remarkable, however, that in mice, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> blocks <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>-induced carrier-mediated dopamine release in the nucleus accumbens and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioural_sensitisation" title="Behavioural sensitisation">behavioural sensitisation</a>, abolishes the <a href="/wiki/Conditioned_place_preference" title="Conditioned place preference">conditioned place preference</a> (CPP) of <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a>, and does not produce reinforcing (or aversive) effects of its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16427030_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16427030-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17905521_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17905521-58">[58]</a></sup> Effects of CPP of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> in rats are mixed, consisting of reinforcement, aversion, and no change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12543228_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12543228-59">[59]</a></sup> In contrast, it is a positive reinforcer in squirrel monkeys,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid408480_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid408480-60">[60]</a></sup> and is well known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">drug of abuse</a> in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9915336_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9915336-61">[61]</a></sup> These discrepancies in response to <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> may reflect species variation or methodological differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17905521_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17905521-58">[58]</a></sup> In human clinical studies, <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> was found to produce mixed responses, similarly to rats, reflecting high subjective individual variability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8462415_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8462415-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11470344_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11470344-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anxiolytic_effect">Anxiolytic effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Anxiolytic effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In behavioural tests of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_(mood)" title="Anxiety (mood)">anxiety</a>, a low dose of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> is an effective <a href="/wiki/Anxiolytic" title="Anxiolytic">anxiolytic</a>, and this anti-anxiety effect is associated with enhanced activity of GABA<sub>A</sub> receptors, as it is partially reversed by <a href="/wiki/GABAA_receptor" title="GABAA receptor">benzodiazepine receptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonists</a>. Mirroring this, animals that have developed tolerance to the anxiolytic effects of <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a> are partially tolerant to <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmanouil_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-emmanouil-64">[64]</a></sup> Indeed, in humans given 30% <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, benzodiazepine receptor antagonists reduced the subjective reports of feeling "high", but did not alter <a href="/wiki/Psychomotor_learning" title="Psychomotor learning">psychomotor</a> performance, in human clinical studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zacny_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-zacny-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analgesic_effect">Analgesic effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Analgesic effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The analgesic effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> are linked to the interaction between the <a href="/wiki/Opioid#Endogenous_opioids" title="Opioid">endogenous opioid</a> system and the descending <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">noradrenergic</a> system. When animals are given <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a> chronically, they develop tolerance to its pain-killing effects, and this also renders the animals tolerant to the analgesic effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Administration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> that bind and block the activity of some endogenous opioids (not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-endorphin" title="Beta-endorphin">β-endorphin</a>) also block the antinociceptive effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-branda_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-branda-67">[67]</a></sup> Drugs that inhibit the breakdown of endogenous opioids also potentiate the antinociceptive effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-branda_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-branda-67">[67]</a></sup> Several experiments have shown that opioid receptor antagonists applied directly to the brain block the antinociceptive effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, but these drugs have no effect when injected into the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>.
</p><p>Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Alpha-2_adrenergic_receptor" title="Alpha-2 adrenergic receptor">α<sub>2</sub>-adrenoceptor</a> antagonists block the pain-reducing effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> when given directly to the spinal cord, but not when applied directly to the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guo_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-guo-68">[68]</a></sup> Indeed, <a href="/wiki/Alpha-2B_adrenergic_receptor" title="Alpha-2B adrenergic receptor">α<sub>2B</sub>-adrenoceptor</a> knockout mice or animals depleted in <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> are nearly completely resistant to the antinociceptive effects of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Apparently <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>-induced release of endogenous opioids causes disinhibition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a> noradrenergic neurons, which release <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> into the spinal cord and inhibit pain signalling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10781114_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10781114-70">[70]</a></sup> Exactly how <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> causes the release of endogenous opioid peptides remains uncertain.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_reactions">Properties and reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Properties and reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nitrous oxide is a colourless, non-toxic gas with a faint, sweet odour.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide supports combustion by releasing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipolar_bond" title="Dipolar bond">dipolar bonded</a> oxygen radical,<sup>[Name?]</sup> thus it can relight a glowing <a href="/wiki/Splint_(laboratory_equipment)" title="Splint (laboratory equipment)">splint</a>.
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> is inert at room temperature and has few reactions. At elevated temperatures, its reactivity increases. For example, nitrous oxide reacts with <span class="chemf nowrap">NaNH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> at 460 K (187 °C) to give <span class="chemf nowrap">NaN<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> :
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">NaNH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">NaN<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + NaOH + <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The above reaction is the route adopted by the commercial chemical industry to produce azide salts, which are used as detonators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InorgChem_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-InorgChem-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The gas was first synthesised in 1772 by English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_philosopher" title="Natural philosopher">natural philosopher</a> and chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a>, who called it <i>phlogisticated nitrous air</i> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phlogiston" title="Phlogiston">phlogiston</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nitrous_Oxide_pioneers_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nitrous_Oxide_pioneers-72">[72]</a></sup> or <i>inflammable nitrous air</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Priestley published his discovery in the book <i>Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air (1775)</i>, where he described how to produce the preparation of "nitrous air diminished", by heating iron filings dampened with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseph_Priestley_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_Priestley-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_use">Early use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Early use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="1239" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg/310px-Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg/465px-Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg/620px-Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laughing_gas_Rumford_Davy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"LIVING MADE EASY"<br/>A satirical print from 1830 depicting <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> administering a dose of laughing gas to a woman</div></div></div>
<p>The first important use of nitrous oxide was made possible by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Beddoes" title="Thomas Beddoes">Thomas Beddoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>, who worked together to publish the book <i>Considerations on the Medical Use and on the Production of Factitious Airs (1794)</i>. This book was important for two reasons. First, James Watt had invented a novel machine to produce "Factitious Airs" (i.e. nitrous oxide) and a novel "breathing apparatus" to inhale the gas. Second, the book also presented the new medical theories by Thomas Beddoes, that <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and other lung diseases could be treated by inhalation of "Factitious Airs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_discovery_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_discovery-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anaesthesia_exhibition,_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2888" data-file-width="3995" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/220px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/330px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/440px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anaesthesia_exhibition,_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>’s <i>Researches chemical and philosophical: chiefly concerning nitrous oxide</i> (1800), pages 556 and 557 (right), outlining potential anaesthetic properties of nitrous oxide in relieving pain during surgery</div></div></div>
<p>The machine to produce "Factitious Airs" had three parts: a furnace to burn the needed material, a vessel with water where the produced gas passed through in a spiral pipe (for impurities to be "washed off"), and finally the gas cylinder with a gasometer where the gas produced, "air", could be tapped into portable air bags (made of airtight oily silk). The breathing apparatus consisted of one of the portable air bags connected with a tube to a mouthpiece. With this new equipment being engineered and produced by 1794, the way was paved for <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which began in 1798 when <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Beddoes" title="Thomas Beddoes">Thomas Beddoes</a> established the <i>"<a href="/wiki/Pneumatic_Institution" title="Pneumatic Institution">Pneumatic Institution</a> for Relieving Diseases by Medical Airs"</i> in <a href="/wiki/Hotwells" title="Hotwells">Hotwells (Bristol)</a>. In the basement of the building, a large-scale machine was producing the gases under the supervision of a young <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, who was encouraged to experiment with new gases for patients to inhale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_discovery_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_discovery-13">[13]</a></sup> The first important work of Davy was examination of the nitrous oxide, and the publication of his results in the book: <i>Researches, Chemical and Philosophical (1800)</i>. In that publication, Davy notes the analgesic effect of nitrous oxide at page 465 and its potential to be used for surgical operations at page 556.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Humphry_Davy_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Humphry_Davy-75">[75]</a></sup> Davy coined the name "laughing gas" for nitrous oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite Davy's discovery that inhalation of nitrous oxide could relieve a conscious person from pain, another 44 years elapsed before doctors attempted to use it for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthesia" title="Anaesthesia">anaesthesia</a>. The use of nitrous oxide as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drug</a> at "laughing gas parties", primarily arranged for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_structure_of_Britain#Upper_class" title="Social structure of Britain">British upper class</a>, became an immediate success beginning in 1799. While the effects of the gas generally make the user appear stuporous, dreamy, and sedated, some people also "get the giggles" in a state of euphoria, and frequently erupt in laughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Illicit_drugs_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Illicit_drugs-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the earliest commercial producers in the U.S. was <a href="/wiki/George_Poe" title="George Poe">George Poe</a>, cousin of the poet <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, who also was the first to liquefy the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaesthetic_use">Anaesthetic use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitrous_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Anaesthetic use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide_and_oxygen" title="Nitrous oxide and oxygen">Nitrous oxide and oxygen</a></div>
<p>The first time nitrous oxide was used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a> drug in the treatment of a patient was when dentist <a href="/wiki/Horace_Wells" title="Horace Wells">Horace Wells</a>, with assistance by <a href="/wiki/Gardner_Quincy_Colton" title="Gardner Quincy Colton">Gardner Quincy Colton</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Mankey_Riggs" title="John Mankey Riggs">John Mankey Riggs</a>, demonstrated insensitivity to pain from a <a href="/wiki/Dental_extraction" title="Dental extraction">dental extraction</a> on 11 December 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_of_Wells_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery_of_Wells-79">[79]</a></sup> In the following weeks, Wells treated the first 12–15 patients with nitrous oxide in <a href="/wiki/Hartford,_Connecticut" title="Hartford, Connecticut">Hartford, Connecticut</a>, and according to his own record, only failed in two cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horace_Wells_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horace_Wells-80">[80]</a></sup> In spite of these convincing results having been reported by Wells to the medical society in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> in December 1844, this new method was not immediately adopted by other dentists. The reason for this was most likely that Wells, in January 1845 at his first public demonstration to the medical faculty in Boston, had been partly unsuccessful, leaving his colleagues doubtful regarding its efficacy and safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_of_anaesthesia_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery_of_anaesthesia-81">[81]</a></sup> The method did not come into general use until 1863, when <a href="/wiki/Gardner_Quincy_Colton" title="Gardner Quincy Colton">Gardner Quincy Colton</a> successfully started to use it in all his "Colton Dental Association" clinics, that he had just established in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Haven" title="New Haven">New Haven</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_discovery_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_discovery-13">[13]</a></sup> Over the following three years, Colton and his associates successfully administered nitrous oxide to more than 25,000 patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-use_in_dentistry_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-use_in_dentistry-14">[14]</a></sup> Today, nitrous oxide is used in dentistry as an <a href="/wiki/Anxiolytic" title="Anxiolytic">anxiolytic</a>, as an adjunct to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_anaesthetic" title="Local anaesthetic">local anaesthetic</a>.
</p><p>Nitrous oxide was not found to be a strong enough anaesthetic for use in major surgery in hospital settings, however.
